<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en"><span dir="auto">Wreck of the RMS <i>Titanic</i></span></h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><div class="flaggedrevs_short plainlinks noprint" id="mw-fr-revisiontag"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic"><img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.25wmf15/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/><img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="//bits.wikimedia.org/static-1.25wmf15/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/></div>
<div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;"><div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">This is the <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">latest accepted revision</a>, <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic">reviewed</a> on <i>9 February 2015</i>.</div>

<th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold"><i>RMS Titanic</i> wreck</th>

<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg"><img alt="Titanic wreck bow.jpg" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="700" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/300px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/450px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg/600px-Titanic_wreck_bow.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>

<th scope="row" style="text-align:left">Location</th>

<th scope="row" style="text-align:left">Operator</th>

</table>
<p>The <b>wreck of the <a href="/wiki/RMS_Titanic" title="RMS Titanic">RMS <i>Titanic</i></a></b> is located about 370 miles (600 km) south-southeast of the coast of <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>, lying at a depth of about 12,500 feet (3,800 m). Over the years since 1912, when the liner hit an iceberg and <a href="/wiki/Sinking_of_the_RMS_Titanic" title="Sinking of the RMS Titanic">sank during her maiden voyage</a>, many impractical, expensive and often physically impossible schemes have been put forward to raise the wreck from its resting place. They have included ideas such as filling the wreck with ping-pong balls, injecting her with 180,000 tons of <a href="/wiki/Vaseline" title="Vaseline">Vaseline</a>, or using half a million tons of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a> to turn her into a giant iceberg that would float back to the surface.</p>
<p>Until 1985, the location of the wreck was unknown. Numerous expeditions tried using <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">sonar</a> to map the sea bed in the hope of spotting the wreck, but failed due to a combination of bad weather, technological difficulties and poor search strategy. The wreck was finally located, 13.2 miles (21.2 km) from the inaccurate position transmitted by <i>Titanic</i> '​s crew while the ship was sinking, by a joint French-American expedition led by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Louis_Michel_(oceanographer)" title="Jean-Louis Michel (oceanographer)">Jean-Louis Michel</a> of <a href="/wiki/IFREMER" title="IFREMER">IFREMER</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</a>. The key to its discovery was an innovative remotely controlled deep-sea vehicle called <a href="/wiki/Argo_(ROV)" title="Argo (ROV)">Argo</a>, which could be towed above the sea bed while its cameras transmitted pictures back to a mother ship.</p>
<p>The wreck lies in two main pieces about a third of a mile (0.6 km) apart. The <a href="/wiki/Bow_(ship)" title="Bow (ship)">bow</a> is still largely recognizable, in spite of its deterioration and the damage it suffered hitting the sea floor, and has a great deal of preserved interiors. The stern is completely ruined due to the damage it suffered while sinking 12,000 feet (3,700 m) and hitting the ocean floor, and is now just a heap of twisted metal, which may explain why it has barely been explored during expeditions to the <i>Titanic</i> wreck. A substantial section of the middle of the ship broke apart and is scattered in chunks across the sea bed. A debris field covering about 5 by 3 miles (8.0 km × 4.8 km) around the wreck contains hundreds of thousands of items spilled from ship as she sank, ranging from passengers' personal effects to machinery, furniture, utensils and coal, as well as fragments of the ship herself. The bodies of the passengers and crew once also lay in the debris field, but have since been entirely consumed by sea creatures, leaving only their shoes lying together in the mud.</p>
<p><i>Titanic</i> '​s wreck has been the focus of intense interest since its discovery and has been visited by numerous expeditions, including salvage operations who have controversially recovered thousands of items which have been conserved and put on public display. The wreckage is too fragile to be raised because its condition has deteriorated in the century it has spent on the sea bottom, and the deterioration has increased since its discovery. Many species of marine animals have made <i>Titanic</i> their home, such as <a href="/wiki/Grenadiers_(fish)" title="Grenadiers (fish)">rattail fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spider_crab" title="Spider crab">spider crabs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brittle_star" title="Brittle star">brittle starfishes</a>. The <i>Titanic</i> also plays host to great communities of <a href="/wiki/Sulfate-reducing_bacteria" title="Sulfate-reducing bacteria">metal-eating bacteria</a>, which as they consume the ship have created <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rusticles" title="Rusticles">rusticles</a> covering most of the hull. The bacteria are slowly devouring <i>Titanic</i> and will gradually reduce her to a spot of rust on the ocean floor with only the remaining scraps of her hull intermingled with her more durable fittings, like the <a href="/wiki/Propeller" title="Propeller">propellers</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telemotor" title="Telemotor">Telemotor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Capstan_(nautical)" title="Capstan (nautical)">Capstans</a>, which can resist attack by microbes.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvaging_Titanic">Salvaging <i>Titanic</i></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Salvaging Titanic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="noviewer thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px">
<div style="position:relative;width:200px;border:1px solid lightgray"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic is located in North Atlantic"><img alt="Wreck of the RMS Titanic is located in North Atlantic" data-file-height="958" data-file-width="1181" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg/200px-North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg/300px-North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg/400px-North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div style="position:absolute;top:43.576%;left:40.083%;height:0;width:0;margin:0;padding:0">
<div style="position:absolute;text-align:center;left:-4px;top:-4px;width:8px;font-size:8px;line-height:0"><img alt="Wreck of the RMS Titanic" data-file-height="64" data-file-width="64" height="8" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/8px-Red_pog.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/12px-Red_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/16px-Red_pog.svg.png 2x" title="Wreck of the RMS Titanic" width="8"/></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_Atlantic_Ocean_laea_relief_location_map.jpg" title="File:North Atlantic Ocean laea relief location map.jpg"></a></div>
Location of the wreck of <i>Titanic</i> in the North Atlantic.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1890" data-file-width="1338" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg/220px-RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg/330px-RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg/440px-RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RMS_Titanic_wreck_depth_to_scale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This image demonstrates the actual depth of the RMS Titanic wreck by contrasting it with a section of lower Manhattan. Objects are to scale.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Soon after <i>Titanic</i> sank on 15 April 1912, proposals were advanced to salvage her from her resting place in the North <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>, as soon as a day after the disaster, despite her location and condition being unknown. The families of several wealthy victims of the disaster – the <a href="/wiki/Guggenheim_family" title="Guggenheim family">Guggenheims</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astor_family" title="Astor family">Astors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Widener_family" title="Widener family">Wideners</a> – formed a consortium and contracted the Merritt and Chapman Derrick and Wrecking Company to raise <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987130_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987130-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> The project was soon abandoned after being deemed impractical. The divers could not even reach a fraction of the necessary depth, where the pressure is over 6,000 pounds per square inch (410 bar). The company considered dropping dynamite on the wreck to dislodge bodies which would float to the surface, but finally gave up after oceanographers suggested that the extreme pressure would have compressed the bodies into gelatinous lumps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWade199272_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWade199272-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> (In fact, this was incorrect. <a href="/wiki/Whale_fall" title="Whale fall">Whale falls</a>, a phenomenon not discovered until 1987 – coincidentally, by the same submersible used for the first manned expedition to <i>Titanic</i> the year before<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELittle2010_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELittle2010-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup> – demonstrate that water-filled corpses, in this case <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a>, can sink to the bottom essentially intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEstes2006298_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEstes2006298-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> The high pressure and cold temperature of the water would have prevented significant quantities of gas forming during <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">decomposition</a>, preventing the bodies of <i>Titanic</i> victims from rising back to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup>)</p>
<p>In later years, various proposals were put forward to salvage <i>Titanic</i>. However, all fell foul due to practical and technological difficulties, a lack of funding and, in many cases, a lack of understanding of the physical conditions at the wreck site. Charles Smith, an architect from Denver, proposed in March 1914 to attach electromagnets to a submarine which would be irresistibly drawn to the wreck's steel hull. Having found its exact position, more electromagnets would be sent down from a fleet of barges which would winch <i>Titanic</i> to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987226_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987226-6"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> An estimated cost of $1.5 million (£35,317,276 today) and its impracticality meant that the idea was not put into practice. Another proposal involved raising <i>Titanic</i> by means of attaching balloons to her hull using electromagnets. Once enough balloons had been attached, the ship would float gently to the surface. Again, the idea got no further than the drawing board.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987227_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987227-7"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The first serious attempt to salvage <i>Titanic</i> took place in July 1953 when <a href="/wiki/Risdon_Beazley" title="Risdon Beazley">Risdon Beazley</a> Ltd, a <a href="/wiki/Southampton" title="Southampton">Southampton</a>-based salvage company, chartered the <a href="/wiki/Admiralty" title="Admiralty">Admiralty</a> Coastal Salvage vessel <i>Help</i> to carry out a secret expedition in the area where <i>Titanic</i> went down. The ship was reported to have carried out underwater blasting, dropping explosives overboard to detonate on the sea bed. It was equipped with deep-sea cameras and remote-controlled machinery for retrieving submerged objects. The expedition's objectives were not publicly disclosed, but its intention seems to have been to blow open the hull of <i>Titanic</i> and retrieve objects from the interior. However, Risdon Beazley failed to find the ship either in 1953 or in a second attempt in 1954.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987228.E2.80.939_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987228.E2.80.939-8"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Salvage_proposals_in_the_1960s_and_1970s">Salvage proposals in the 1960s and 1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Salvage proposals in the 1960s and 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="300" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg/170px-Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4e/Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg/255px-Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/4/4e/Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raise_The_Titanic_Movie_Poster.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Titanic</i> surfacing in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Raise_the_Titanic_(film)" title="Raise the Titanic (film)">Raise the Titanic</a></i>; the scene depicted was physically impossible.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the mid-1960s, a hosiery worker from <a href="/wiki/Baldock" title="Baldock">Baldock</a> named Douglas Woolley devised a plan to find <i>Titanic</i> using a <a href="/wiki/Bathyscaphe" title="Bathyscaphe">bathyscaphe</a> (like <i><a href="/wiki/Bathyscaphe_Trieste" title="Bathyscaphe Trieste">Trieste</a></i>, used to reach the bottom of the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Trench" title="Mariana Trench">Mariana Trench</a> in 1960) and raise the wreck by inflating nylon balloons that would be attached to her hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The declared objective was to "bring the wreck into Liverpool and convert it to a floating museum."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994302_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994302-10"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> The Titanic Salvage Company was established to manage the scheme and a group of businessmen from <a href="/wiki/West_Berlin" title="West Berlin">West Berlin</a> set up an entity called Titanic-Tresor to support it financially.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> It fell apart when its proponents found they could not overcome the problem of how the balloons would be inflated in the first place. Calculations showed that it could take ten years to generate enough gas to overcome the water pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987230.E2.80.931_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987230.E2.80.931-11"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A variety of audacious but equally impractical schemes was put forward during the 1970s. One proposal called for 180,000 tons of molten wax (or alternatively, <a href="/wiki/Vaseline" title="Vaseline">Vaseline</a>) to be pumped into <i>Titanic</i>, lifting her to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987231_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987231-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> Another proposal involved filling <i>Titanic</i> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ping-pong_ball" title="Ping-pong ball">ping-pong balls</a>, but overlooked the fact that the balls would be crushed flat by the pressure long before reaching <i>Titanic</i> '​s depth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESerwayJewett2006494_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESerwayJewett2006494-13"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> A similar idea involving the use of Benthos glass spheres,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Benthos_glass_spheres_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Benthos_glass_spheres-14"><span>[</span>a<span>]</span></a></sup> which could survive the pressure, was scuppered when the cost of the number of spheres required was put at over $238 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987231_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987231-12"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> An unemployed haulage contractor from <a href="/wiki/Walsall" title="Walsall">Walsall</a> named Arthur Hickey proposed to turn <i>Titanic</i> into an iceberg, freezing the water around the wreck to encase it in a buoyant jacket of ice. This, being lighter than liquid water, would float to the surface and could be towed to shore. <a href="/wiki/The_BOC_Group" title="The BOC Group">The BOC Group</a> calculated that this would require half a million tons of liquid nitrogen to be pumped down to the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENew_Scientist6_October_1977_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENew_Scientist6_October_1977-15"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup> In his 1976 thriller <i><a href="/wiki/Raise_the_Titanic!" title="Raise the Titanic!">Raise the Titanic!</a></i>, author <a href="/wiki/Clive_Cussler" title="Clive Cussler">Clive Cussler</a>'s hero <a href="/wiki/Dirk_Pitt" title="Dirk Pitt">Dirk Pitt</a> repairs the holes in <i>Titanic</i> '​s hull, pumps it full of compressed air and succeeds in making it "leap out of the waves like a modern submarine blowing its ballast tanks", a scene depicted on the posters of the subsequent film of the book. Although this was an "artistically stimulating" highlight of the film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuid1996210_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuid1996210-16"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> made using a 55 ft (17 m) model of <i>Titanic</i>, it would not have been physically possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHicksKropf2002194_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHicksKropf2002194-17"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</a> had long been interested in finding <i>Titanic</i>. Although early negotiations with possible backers were abandoned when it emerged that they wanted to turn the wreck into souvenir paperweights, more sympathetic backers joined Ballard to form a company named Seasonics International Ltd as a vehicle for rediscovering and exploring <i>Titanic</i>. In October 1977 he made his first attempt to find the ship with the aid of the <a href="/wiki/Alcoa" title="Alcoa">Alcoa</a> corporation's deep sea salvage vessel <i>Seaprobe</i>. This was essentially a <a href="/wiki/Drillship" title="Drillship">drillship</a> with sonar equipment and cameras attached to the end of the drilling pipe. It could lift objects from the seabed using a remote-controlled mechanical claw.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198738_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198738-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> The expedition ended in failure when the drilling pipe broke, sending 3,000 feet (910 m) of pipe and $600,000 worth of electronics plunging to the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198738_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198738-18"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1978, <a href="/wiki/The_Walt_Disney_Company" title="The Walt Disney Company">The Walt Disney Company</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(magazine)" title="National Geographic (magazine)">National Geographic</a></i> magazine considered mounting a joint expedition to find <i>Titanic</i>, using the aluminium submersible <a href="/wiki/Aluminaut" title="Aluminaut">Aluminaut</a>. <i>Titanic</i> would have been well within the submersible's depth limits, but the plans were abandoned for financial reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The following year, the British billionaire financier and tycoon Sir <a href="/wiki/James_Goldsmith" title="James Goldsmith">James Goldsmith</a> set up Seawise &amp; Titanic Salvage Ltd with the involvement of underwater diving and photographic experts. His aim was to use the publicity of finding <i>Titanic</i> to promote his newly established magazine, <i><a href="/wiki/NOW!_(1979%E2%80%9381_magazine)" title="NOW! (1979–81 magazine)">NOW!</a></i>. An expedition to the North Atlantic was scheduled for 1980 but was cancelled due to financial difficulties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987132-9"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> A year later, <i>NOW!</i> folded after 84 issues with Goldsmith incurring huge financial losses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27.2C_11_May_1981_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTE.27.27Time.27.27.2C_11_May_1981-19"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Fred Koehler, an electronics repairman from <a href="/wiki/Coral_Gables,_Florida" title="Coral Gables, Florida">Coral Gables, Florida</a>, sold his electronics shop to finance the completion of a two-man deep-sea submersible called <i>Seacopter</i>. He planned to dive to <i>Titanic</i>, enter the hull and retrieve a fabulous collection of diamonds rumored to be contained in the purser's safe. However, he was unable to obtain financial backing for his planned expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994304_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994304-20"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup> Another proposal involved using a semi-submersible platform mounted with cranes, resting on two watertight <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supertanker" title="Supertanker">supertankers</a>, that would winch the wreck off the seabed and carry it to shore. A proponent was quoted as saying, "It's like the Great Wall of China – given enough time and money and people, you can do anything." Time, money and people were not forthcoming and the proposal got no further than any of its predecessors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987137_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1987137-21"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jack_Grimm.27s_expeditions.2C_1980.E2.80.9383">Jack Grimm's expeditions, 1980–83</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Jack Grimm's expeditions, 1980–83">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 17 July 1980, an expedition sponsored by Texan oilman Jack Grimm set off from <a href="/wiki/Port_Everglades" title="Port Everglades">Port Everglades</a>, Florida, in the research vessel <i>H.J.W. Fay</i>. Grimm had previously sponsored expeditions to find <a href="/wiki/Noah%27s_Ark" title="Noah's Ark">Noah's Ark</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Loch_Ness_Monster" title="Loch Ness Monster">Loch Ness Monster</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bigfoot" title="Bigfoot">Bigfoot</a>, and the giant hole in the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> predicted by the pseudoscientific <a href="/wiki/Hollow_Earth" title="Hollow Earth">Hollow Earth</a> hypothesis. To raise funds for his <i>Titanic</i> expedition, he obtained sponsorship from friends with whom he played poker, sold media rights through the <a href="/wiki/William_Morris_Agency" title="William Morris Agency">William Morris Agency</a>, commissioned a book, and obtained the services of <a href="/wiki/Orson_Welles" title="Orson Welles">Orson Welles</a> to narrate a documentary. He acquired scientific support from <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a> by donating $330,000 to the <a href="/wiki/Lamont%E2%80%93Doherty_Earth_Observatory" title="Lamont–Doherty Earth Observatory">Lamont–Doherty Geological Observatory</a> for the purchase of a wide-sweep sonar, in exchange for five years' use of the equipment and the services of technicians to support it. Drs. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=William_B._Ryan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="William B. Ryan (page does not exist)">William B. Ryan</a> of Columbia University and <a href="/wiki/Fred_Spiess" title="Fred Spiess">Fred Spiess</a> of <a href="/wiki/Scripps_Institution_of_Oceanography" title="Scripps Institution of Oceanography">Scripps Institution of Oceanography</a> in California joined the expedition as consultants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELord1987232.E2.80.933_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELord1987232.E2.80.933-22"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> They nearly stayed ashore when Grimm introduced them to a supposed fellow consultant – a monkey called Titan, which was trained to point at a spot on the map to supposedly indicate where <i>Titanic</i> was. The scientists issued an ultimatum: "It's either us or the monkey." Grimm preferred the monkey, but was prevailed upon to leave it behind and bring the scientists instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198747_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198747-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The results were inconclusive, as three weeks of surveying in almost continuous bad weather during July and August 1980 failed to find <i>Titanic</i>. The problem was exacerbated by technological limitations; the Sea MARC sonar used by the expedition had a relatively low resolution and was a new and untested piece of equipment. It was nearly lost only 36 hours after it was first deployed when the tail was ripped off during a sharp turn, destroying the <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">magnetometer</a>, which would have been vital for detecting <i>Titanic</i> '​s hull. Nonetheless it managed to survey an area of some 500 square nautical miles and identified 14 possible targets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198747_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198747-23"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Grimm mounted a second expedition in June 1981 aboard the research vessel <i>Gyre</i>, with Spiess and Ryan again joining the expedition. To increase their chances of finding the wreck, the team employed a much more capable sonar device, the Scripps Deep Tow. The weather was again very poor, but all 14 of the targets were successfully covered and found to be natural features. On the last day of the expedition, an object that looked like a propeller was found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198750_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198750-24"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup> Grimm announced on his return to Boston that <i>Titanic</i> had been found, but the scientists declined to endorse his identification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198751_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198751-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In July 1983, Grimm went back a third time with Ryan aboard the research vessel <i>Robert D. Conrad</i> to have another look at the "propeller". This time nothing was found and very bad weather brought an early end to the expedition. It later turned out that Sea MARC had actually passed over <i>Titanic</i> but had failed to detect it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198751_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198751-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> while Deep Tow passed within 1.5 miles (2.4 km) of the wreck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198749_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198749-26"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Ballard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="324" data-file-width="220" height="250" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Robert_Ballard.jpg/170px-Robert_Ballard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Robert_Ballard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/af/Robert_Ballard.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Robert_Ballard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dr. Robert D. Ballard, the discoverer in September 1985 of the wreck of <i>Titanic</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>D. Michael Harris and Jack Grimm had failed to find <i>Titanic</i> but their expeditions did succeed in producing fairly detailed mapping of the area in which the ship had sunk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198751_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198751-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> It was clear that the position given in <i>Titanic</i> '​s distress signals was incorrect; the problem was finding the wreck, a relatively small target in a very large search area. Despite the failure of his 1977 expedition, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Ballard" title="Robert Ballard">Robert Ballard</a> had not given up hope and devised new technologies and a new search strategy to tackle the problem. The new technology was a system called <i>Argo / Jason</i>. This consisted of a remotely controlled deep-sea vehicle called <i>Argo</i>, equipped with sonar and cameras and towed behind a ship, with a robot called <i>Jason</i> tethered to it that could roam the sea floor, take close-up images and gather specimens. The images from the system would be transmitted back to a control room on the towing vessel where they could be assessed immediately. Although it was designed for scientific purposes, it also had important military applications and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a> agreed to sponsor the system's development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198753_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198753-27"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup> on condition that it was to be used to carry out a number of programmes – many still classified – for the Navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard2008100_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard2008100-28"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Navy commissioned Ballard and his team to carry out a month-long expedition every year for four years, to keep <i>Argo / Jason</i> in good working condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002235_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002235-29"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup> It agreed to Ballard's proposal to use some of the time to search for <i>Titanic</i> once the Navy's objectives had been met; the search would provide an ideal opportunity to test <i>Argo / Jason</i>. In 1984 the Navy sent Ballard and <i>Argo</i> to map the wrecks of the sunken nuclear submarines <a href="/wiki/USS_Thresher_(SSN-593)" title="USS Thresher (SSN-593)">USS <i>Thresher</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Scorpion_(SSN-589)" title="USS Scorpion (SSN-589)">USS <i>Scorpion</i></a>, lost in the North Atlantic at depths of up to 9,800 feet (3,000 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard200897_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard200897-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> The expedition found the submarines and made an important discovery. As <i>Thresher</i> and <i>Scorpion</i> sank, debris spilled out from them across a wide area of the seabed and was sorted by the currents, so that light debris drifted furthest away from the site of the sinking. This "debris field" was far larger than the wrecks themselves. By following the comet-like trail of debris, the main pieces of wreckage could be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002225_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002225-31"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A second expedition to map the wreck of <i>Scorpion</i> was mounted in 1985. Only twelve days of search time would be left at the end of the expedition to look for <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard200897_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard200897-30"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> As Harris/Grimm's unsuccessful efforts had taken more than forty days,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198751_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198751-25"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup> Ballard decided that extra help would be needed. He approached the French national oceanographic agency, <a href="/wiki/IFREMER" title="IFREMER">IFREMER</a>, with which Woods Hole had previously collaborated. The agency had recently developed a high-resolution side-scan sonar called SAR and agreed to send a research vessel, <i><a href="/wiki/RV_Le_Suro%C3%AEt" title="RV Le Suroît">Le Suroît</a></i>, to survey the sea bed in the area where <i>Titanic</i> was believed to lie. The idea was for the French to use the sonar to find likely targets, and then for the Americans to use <i>Argo</i> to check out the targets and hopefully confirm whether they were in fact the wreck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002239_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002239-32"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The French team spent five weeks, from 5 July to 12 August 1985, "mowing the lawn" – sailing back and forth across the 150-square-mile (390 km<sup>2</sup>) target area to scan the sea bed in a series of stripes. However, they found nothing, though it turned out that they had passed within a few hundred yards of <i>Titanic</i> in their first run.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard200898_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard200898-33"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Ballard realized that looking for the wreck itself using sonar was unlikely to be successful and adopted a different tactic, drawing on the experience of the surveys of <i>Thresher</i> and <i>Scorpion</i>; he would look for the debris field rather than the wreck itself,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard2008101_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard2008101-34"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> using <i>Argo</i> '​s cameras rather than sonar. Whereas sonar could not distinguish man-made debris on the sea bed from natural objects, cameras could. The debris field would also be a far bigger target, stretching a mile (1.5 km) or longer, whereas <i>Titanic</i> itself was only 90 feet (27 m) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198766_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198766-35"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup> The search required round-the-clock towing of <i>Argo</i> back and forth above the sea bed, with shifts of watchers aboard the research vessel <i>Knorr</i> looking at the camera pictures for any sign of debris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002250_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallardHively2002250-36"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> After a week of fruitless searching, at 12.48 am on Sunday 1 September 1985 pieces of debris began to appear on <i>Knorr</i> '​s screens. One of them was identified as a boiler, identical to those shown in pictures from 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198782_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198782-37"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> The following day, the main part of the wreck was found and <i>Argo</i> sent back the first pictures of <i>Titanic</i> since her sinking 73 years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198788_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198788-38"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> The discovery made headlines around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard198798_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard198798-39"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Subsequent_expeditions">Subsequent expeditions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Subsequent expeditions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="1986.E2.80.931998">1986–1998</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1986–1998">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ALVIN_submersible.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1197" data-file-width="930" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/ALVIN_submersible.jpg/220px-ALVIN_submersible.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/ALVIN_submersible.jpg/330px-ALVIN_submersible.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/ALVIN_submersible.jpg/440px-ALVIN_submersible.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ALVIN_submersible.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>DSV Alvin</i>, used in 1986 to mount the first manned expedition to the wreck of <i>Titanic</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following his discovery of the wreck site, Ballard returned to <i>Titanic</i> in July 1986 aboard the research vessel <a href="/wiki/RV_Atlantis_II" title="RV Atlantis II">RV <i>Atlantis II</i></a>. Now the deep-diving submersible <a href="/wiki/DSV_Alvin" title="DSV Alvin">DSV <i>Alvin</i></a> could take people back to <i>Titanic</i> for the first time since her sinking, and the <a href="/wiki/Remotely_operated_vehicle" title="Remotely operated vehicle">remotely operated vehicle</a> (ROV) <i><a href="/wiki/Jason_Jr." title="Jason Jr.">Jason Jr.</a></i> would allow the explorers to investigate the interior of the wreck. Another system, <a href="/wiki/Acoustically_Navigated_Geological_Underwater_Survey" title="Acoustically Navigated Geological Underwater Survey">ANGUS</a>, was used to carry out photo surveys of the debris field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994309_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994309-40"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Jason Jr.</i> descended the ruined Grand Staircase as far as B Deck, and photographed remarkably well preserved interiors, including some chandeliers still hanging from the ceilings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1992207_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1992207-41"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Between 25 July and 10 September 1987, an expedition mounted by IFREMER and a consortium of American investors which included George Tulloch, G. Michael Harris, D. Michael Harris and Ralph White made 32 dives to <i>Titanic</i> using the submersible <i><a href="/wiki/Nautile" title="Nautile">Nautile</a></i>. Controversially, they salvaged and brought ashore more than 1,800 objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994310-42"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> A joint Russian-Canadian-American expedition took place in 1991 using the research vessel <i><a href="/wiki/Akademik_Mstislav_Keldysh" title="Akademik Mstislav Keldysh">Akademik Mstislav Keldysh</a></i> and its two <i><a href="/wiki/Mir_(submersible)" title="Mir (submersible)">MIR</a></i> submersibles. Sponsored by Stephen Low and <a href="/wiki/IMAX" title="IMAX">IMAX</a>, <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_(magazine)" title="National Geographic (magazine)">National Geographic</a></i> and others, the expedition carried out extensive scientific research with a crew of 130 scientists and engineers. The <i>MIRs</i> carried out 17 dives, spending over 140 hours at the bottom, shooting 40,000 feet (12,000 m) of IMAX film. This was used to create the 1995 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Titanica" title="Titanica">Titanica</a></i>, which was later released in the US on DVD in a re-edited version narrated by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Nimoy" title="Leonard Nimoy">Leonard Nimoy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994312.E2.80.933_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994312.E2.80.933-43"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1992209_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1992209-44"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>IFREMER and RMS Titanic Inc., the successors to the sponsors of the 1987 expedition, returned to the wreck with <i>Nautile</i> and the ROV <i>Robin</i> in June 1993. Over the course of fifteen days, <i>Nautile</i> made fifteen dives lasting between eight and twelve hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994314.E2.80.936_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994314.E2.80.936-45"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup> Another 800 artefacts were recovered during the expedition including a two-tonne piece of a reciprocating engine, a lifeboat <a href="/wiki/Davit" title="Davit">davit</a> and the steam whistle from the ship's forward funnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994324_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEatonHaas1994324-46"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1993, 1994, 1996, 1998 and 2000, RMS Titanic Inc. carried out an intensive series of dives that led to the recovery of over 4,000 items in the first two expeditions alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998216_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998216-47"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> The 1996 expedition controversially attempted to raise a section of <i>Titanic</i> itself, a section of the outer hull that originally comprised part of the wall of two First Class cabins on C Deck, extending down to D Deck. It weighed 20 tons, measured 15 by 25 feet (4.6 m × 7.6 m) and had four portholes in it, three of which still had glass in them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012254_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012254-48"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The section had come loose either during the sinking or as a result of the impact with the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012277_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012277-49"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> Its recovery using diesel-filled floatation bags was turned into something of an entertainment event with two cruise ships accompanying the expedition to the wreck site. Passengers were offered the chance, at $5,000 per person, to watch the recovery on television screens in their cabins while enjoying luxury accommodation, Las Vegas-style shows and casino gambling aboard the ships. Various celebrities were recruited to enliven the proceedings, including <a href="/wiki/Burt_Reynolds" title="Burt Reynolds">Burt Reynolds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Debbie_Reynolds" title="Debbie Reynolds">Debbie Reynolds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buzz_Aldrin" title="Buzz Aldrin">Buzz Aldrin</a>, and "Grand Receptions" for VIPs were scheduled on-shore where the hull section would be displayed. However, the lift ended disastrously when rough weather caused the ropes supporting the bags to snap. At the moment the ropes broke, the hull section had been lifted to within only 200 feet (61 m) of the surface. It hurtled 12,000 feet (3,700 m) back down,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998217.E2.80.938_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998217.E2.80.938-50"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> embedding itself upright on the sea floor. Although the attempt was strongly criticised by marine archaeologists, scientists and historians as a money-making publicity stunt, a second, successful attempt to lift the fragment was carried out in 1998. The so-called "Big Piece" was conserved in a laboratory in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Fe,_New_Mexico" title="Santa Fe, New Mexico">Santa Fe</a> for two years before being put on display at the <a href="/wiki/Luxor_Las_Vegas" title="Luxor Las Vegas">Luxor Las Vegas</a> hotel and casino.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012258_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012258-51"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995, Canadian director <a href="/wiki/James_Cameron" title="James Cameron">James Cameron</a> chartered the <i>Akademik Mstislav Keldysh</i> and the <i>MIRs</i> to make 12 dives to <i>Titanic</i>. He used the resulting footage to considerable effect in his blockbuster 1997 film <i><a href="/wiki/Titanic_(1997_film)" title="Titanic (1997 film)">Titanic</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacInnisCameron200523_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacInnisCameron200523-52"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup> The discovery of the wreck in 1985 and a <i>National Geographic</i> documentary of Ballard's 1986 expedition had inspired Cameron to write a synopsis in 1987 of what eventually became the film <i>Titanic</i>: "Do story with bookends of present day scene of wreck using submersibles intercut with memories of a survivor and re-created scenes of the night of the sinking. A crucible of human values under stress."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParisi19988_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParisi19988-53"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup> Cameron's expedition did not salvage anything from the ship.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="2000_to_present">2000 to present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2000 to present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg/220px-Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg/330px-Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg/440px-Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Captain_Smith%27s_bathroom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The partly collapsed bathroom of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Smith_(sea_captain)" title="Edward Smith (sea captain)">Captain Edward Smith</a>, with the bathtub now filled with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rusticles" title="Rusticles">rusticles</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 2000 expedition by RMS Titanic Inc. carried out 28 dives during which over 800 artefacts were recovered, including the ship's engine telegraphs, perfume vials and watertight door gears.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2000_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2000-54"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2001, an American couple – David Leibowitz and Kimberly Miller<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> – caused controversy when they were married aboard a submersible that had set down on the bow of <i>Titanic</i>, in a deliberate echo of a famous scene from James Cameron's 1997 film. The wedding was essentially a publicity stunt, sponsored by a British company called SubSea Explorer which had offered a free dive to <i>Titanic</i> that Leibowitz had won. He asked whether his fiancée could come too and was told that she could – but only if she agreed to get married during the trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012239_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpignesi2012239-56"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> The same company also brought along Philip Littlejohn, the grandson of one of <i>Titanic</i> '​s surviving crew members, who became the first relative of a <i>Titanic</i> passenger or crew member to visit the wreck.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2001_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2001-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Cameron himself also returned to <i>Titanic</i> in 2001 to carry out filming for <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/Ghosts_of_the_Abyss" title="Ghosts of the Abyss">Ghosts of the Abyss</a></i>, filmed in <a href="/wiki/3D_film" title="3D film">3D</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2001_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2001-57"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003 and 2004, the US <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> carried out two expeditions to <i>Titanic</i>. The first, carried out between 22 June and 2 July 2003, performed four dives in two days. Its key aims were to assess the current condition of the wreck site and carry out scientific observations to support ongoing research. The stern section, which had previously received relatively little attention from explorers, was specifically targeted for analysis. The microbial colonies aboard <i>Titanic</i> were also a key focus of investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.M.S._Titanic_Expedition_2003_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.M.S._Titanic_Expedition_2003-58"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> The second expedition, from 27 May – 12 June 2004, saw the return of Robert Ballard to <i>Titanic</i> nearly 20 years after he discovered it. The expedition spent 11 days on the wreck, carrying out high-resolution mapping using video and stereoscopic still images.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.M.S._Titanic_Expedition_2004_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.M.S._Titanic_Expedition_2004-59"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>2005 saw two expeditions to the <i>Titanic</i>. James Cameron returned to the <i>Titanic</i> for the third and last time to film <i>Last Mysteries of the Titanic</i>, as the <i>Titanic</i>, and has claimed that this will wrap up <i>my 10-year odyssey with the</i> Titanic<i>, and I won't be coming back here</i>, because the <i>Titanic</i>, having been underwater for 93 years, was showing very severe signs of deterioration in the foremast, the boat deck, the Marconi rooms, and the stern's condition was explained via NOAA, because in his 1995 and 2005 expeditions to the <i>Titanic</i>, Cameron did not explore the stern of the wreck due to this part of the <i>Titanic</i> being in even worse condition than the bow. It also saw another expedition, <i>Titanic's Final Moments: Missing Pieces</i>, which began looking for pieces of the <i>Titanic</i> never before seen in the debris field.</p>
<p>RMS Titanic Inc. mounted further expeditions to <i>Titanic</i> in 2004<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2004_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETimeline_for_2004-60"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> and 2010, when the first comprehensive map of the entire debris field was produced. Two <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_underwater_vehicle" title="Autonomous underwater vehicle">autonomous underwater vehicles</a> – torpedo-shaped robots – repeatedly ran backwards and forwards across the 3 by 5 miles (4.8 km × 8.0 km) debris field, taking sonar scans and over 130,000 high-resolution images. This enabled a detailed photomosaic of the debris field to be created for the first time, giving scientists a much clearer view of the dynamics of the ship's sinking. Several hiccups happened during this expedition, as several hurricanes were passing over the wreck site, and the team got the <i>Remora</i> ROV caught in a piece of wreckage in the <i>Titanic</i>s expansion joint, and carefully had to get the robot out of the expansion joint to avoid damaging the <i>Titanic</i>, as the <i>Titanic</i>, having been underwater for 98 years, was in very fragile condition, and one of the AUVs, Ginger, was weighed down by a piece of the lead anchor discovered in 2005. This same year saw the discovery of the new bacteria living in the rusticles on the <i>Titanic</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Halomonas_titanicae" title="Halomonas titanicae">Halomonas titanicae</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECanfield8_March_2012_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECanfield8_March_2012-61"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Tourist and scientific visits to <i>Titanic</i> are still continuing; by April 2012, 100 years since the disaster and nearly 25 since the discovery of the wreck, around 140 people had visited.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESymondsApril_2012_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESymondsApril_2012-62"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> On 14 April 2012 (the 100th anniversary of the ship's sinking), the wreck of the <i>Titanic</i> became eligible for protection as a UNESCO <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>, and in the same month, Robert Ballard, the wreck's discoverer, has announced of a plan to preserve the wreck of the <i>Titanic</i> by using deep-sea robots to paint the wreck with <a href="/wiki/Anti-fouling_paint" title="Anti-fouling paint">anti-fouling paint</a>, to help keep the wreck in its current state for all time. The proposed plan that Ballard announced has been outlined in a documentary made to time with the Titanic's 100th sinking anniversary called <i>Save the Titanic With Bob Ballard</i> where Ballard himself talks about how this proposed paint job on the wreck will work. Ballard says that the reason he has proposed to robotically clean and repaint the <i>Titanic</i> with a colour scheme that mimics the rusticles is because when <i>I visited the</i> Titanic <i>in 1986, I saw original anti-fouling paint on the ship's hull which was still working even after 74 years on the seabed</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natgeo_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-natgeo-63"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description_of_the_wreck">Description of the wreck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Description of the wreck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The position of the wreck is a considerable distance from the location transmitted by the ship's wireless operators before she went down. The initial location was given as <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_44_N_50_24_W_type:event&amp;title=RMS+Titanic%27s+initial+reported+position"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°44′N</span> <span class="longitude">50°24′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.733°N 50.400°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.733; -50.400</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">RMS Titanic's initial reported position</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>, 20.8 miles (33.5 km) from the wreck. A corrected location was later transmitted as <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_46_N_50_14_W_type:event&amp;title=RMS+Titanic%27s+corrected+reported+position"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°46′N</span> <span class="longitude">50°14′W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.767°N 50.233°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.767; -50.233</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">RMS Titanic's corrected reported position</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>, but this too was inaccurate, by 13.2 miles (21.2 km). <i>Titanic</i> is in two main pieces 370 miles (600 km) south-east of <a href="/wiki/Mistaken_Point,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Mistaken Point, Newfoundland and Labrador">Mistaken Point</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland</a>. The bow is located at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_43_57_N_49_56_49_W_type:event&amp;title=Position+of+RMS+Titanic%27s+bow+section"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°43′57″N</span> <span class="longitude">49°56′49″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.73250°N 49.94694°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.73250; -49.94694</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Position of RMS Titanic's bow section</span>)</span></span></span></a></span><span style="font-size: small;"><span id="coordinates"><a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">Coordinates</a>: <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_43_57_N_49_56_49_W_type:event&amp;title=Position+of+RMS+Titanic%27s+bow+section"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°43′57″N</span> <span class="longitude">49°56′49″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.73250°N 49.94694°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.73250; -49.94694</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Position of RMS Titanic's bow section</span>)</span></span></span></a></span></span></span> and the stern is about 1,970 feet (600 m) to the south at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_43_35_N_49_56_54_W_type:event&amp;title=Position+of+RMS+Titanic%27s+stern+section"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°43′35″N</span> <span class="longitude">49°56′54″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.72639°N 49.94833°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.72639; -49.94833</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Position of RMS Titanic's stern section</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>. The boilers found by <i>Argo</i>, which mark the point at which the ship went down,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGibson2012240_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGibson2012240-64"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> are about 600 feet (180 m) east of the stern at <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion"><a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;params=41_43_32_N_49_56_49_W_type:event&amp;title=Position+of+RMS+Titanic+boilers+located+by+Argo"><span class="geo-default"><span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location"><span class="latitude">41°43′32″N</span> <span class="longitude">49°56′49″W</span></span></span><span class="geo-multi-punct">﻿ / ﻿</span><span class="geo-nondefault"><span class="vcard"><span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">41.72556°N 49.94694°W</span><span style="display:none">﻿ / <span class="geo">41.72556; -49.94694</span></span><span style="display:none">﻿ (<span class="fn org">Position of RMS Titanic boilers located by Argo</span>)</span></span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126.E2.80.937_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126.E2.80.937-65"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The two main parts of the wreck of <i>Titanic</i> present a striking contrast. Although fourteen survivors testified that the ship had broken apart as she sank, this testimony was discounted by the official inquiries, and it was supposed that the ship had sunk intact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011126-66"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> It is now clear that the stresses on <i>Titanic</i> caused the ship to split apart between the second and third funnels at or just below the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bow_section">Bow section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Bow section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The bow section, which measures about 470 feet (140 m) long, is thought to have descended at an angle of about 45°. Its distance from the stern was caused by its planing forward horizontally by about 1 foot (0.30 m) for every 6 feet (1.8 m) of its descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011127_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011127-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> During the descent to the sea bed, the funnels were swept away, taking with them the rigging and large lengths of cables. These dragged along the boat deck, tearing away many of the davits and much of the other deck equipment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1992205_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1992205-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The foremast was also torn down, falling onto the port bridge area. The ship's wheelhouse was swept away, possibly after being hit by the falling foremast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The bow hit the bottom at a speed of about 20 knots (23 mph; 37 km/h), digging about 60 feet (18 m) deep into the mud, up to the base of the anchors. The impact bent the hull in two places and caused it to buckle downwards by about 10° under the forward well deck cranes and by about 4° under the forward <a href="/wiki/Expansion_joint" title="Expansion joint">expansion joint</a>. When the bow section hit the sea bed, the weakened decks at the rear, where the ship had broken apart, collapsed on top of each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011127_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011127-68"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup> The forward hatch cover was also blown off and landed a couple of hundred feet in front of the bow, possibly due to the force of water being pushed out as the bow impacted the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The area around the bridge is particularly badly damaged; as Robert Ballard has put it, it looks "as if it had been squashed by a giant's fist".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987206-71"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Pellegrino" title="Charles R. Pellegrino">Charles R. Pellegrino</a> has proposed that this was the result of a "down-blast" of water, caused by a slipstream that had followed the bow section as it fell towards the sea bed. According to Pellegrino's hypothesis, when the bow came to an abrupt halt the inertia of the slipstream caused a rapidly moving column of water weighing thousands of tons to strike the top of the wreck, striking it near the bridge. The roof of the officers' quarters and the sides of the gymnasium were pushed in, railings were blown outwards and vertical steel columns supporting the decks were bent into a C-shape. Large parts of the bow's interior were demolished by surges of water and violent eddies kicked up by the wreck's sudden halt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012100.E2.80.931_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012100.E2.80.931-72"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> The actual damage made by the iceberg is not visible at the bow as it is buried under mud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGannonFebruary_1995_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGannonFebruary_1995-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stern_section">Stern section</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Stern section">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The stern section, which measures about 350 feet (110 m) long, suffered catastrophic damage during the descent and in landing on the sea bed. It had not fully filled with water when it sank and the increasing water pressure caused trapped air pockets to implode, tearing apart the hull. Data from a sonar map made during a 2010 expedition showed that the stern section rotated like a helicopter blade as it sank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> The rudder appears to have swung over to an angle of about 30–45° during the stern's descent, causing the section to follow a tight spiral to the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012107_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012107-75"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup> It probably struck rudder-first, burying most of the rudder in the mud up to a depth of 50 feet (15 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012108_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012108-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> The decks pancaked on top of each other and the hull plating splayed out to the sides of the shattered section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987204-67"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> The pancaking is so severe that the combined height of the decks, which are piled up on top of the reciprocating engines, is now generally not more than about 12 to 15 feet (3.7 to 4.6 m) high. No individual deck is more than about 1 foot (30 cm) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012108_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPellegrino2012108-76"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Large sections of the hull plating seem to have fallen off well before the wreck hit the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011128_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011128-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup> One such section, thought to have been from the galleys, separated from the stern in one piece and landed nearby.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELynch1992205_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELynch1992205-69"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> The force of the water tore up the poop deck and folded it back on itself. The center propeller is totally buried, while the force of the impact caused the two wing propellers and shafts to be bent upwards by an angle of about 20°.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011128_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHalpernWeeks2011128-77"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A large V-shaped section of the ship just aft of midships, running from the keel upwards through Number 1 Boiler Room and upwards to cover the area under the third and fourth funnels, was believed to have disintegrated entirely when the ship broke up. This was one of the weakest parts of the ship as a result of the presence of two large open spaces – the forward end of the engine room and the aft First Class passenger staircase. The rest of this part of the ship are scattered across the seabed at distances of 130 to 260 feet (40 to 79 m) from the main part of the stern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987202_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987202-78"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> During the 2010 expedition to map the wreck site, a major chunk of the deck house (the base of the third funnel) along with pieces of the third funnel was found. This showed that instead of simply disintegrating into a mass of debris, large sections of the ship broke off in chunks and that the ship broke in half between the second and third funnels, not the third and fourth funnels. Five of the boilers from Number 1 Boiler Room came loose during its disintegration and landed in the debris field around the stern. Experts believe that this tight cluster of the boilers marks the hypocenter (ground zero) of where the ship broke up 12,000 feet above.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> The rest of the boilers are still presumably located in the bow section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987190_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987190-80"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Debris_fields">Debris fields</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Debris fields">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As Titanic broke apart, many objects and pieces of hull were scattered across the sea bed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> There are two debris fields in the vicinity of the wreck, each between 2,000–2,600 ft (610–729 m) long, trailing in a south-westerly direction from the bow and stern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987207-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> They cover an area of about 2 square miles (5.2 km<sup>2</sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubin_1987_p65-69_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin_1987_p65-69-81"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> Most of the debris is concentrated near the stern section of <i>Titanic</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987150_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987150-82"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> It consists of thousands of objects from the interior of the ship, ranging from tons of coal spilled from ruptured bunkers to suitcases, clothes, corked wine bottles (many still intact despite the pressure), bathtubs, windows, washbasins, jugs, bowls, hand mirrors and numerous other personal effects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBallard1987203_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBallard1987203-83"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> The debris field also includes numerous pieces of the ship itself, with the largest pieces of debris in the vicinity of the partially disintegrated stern section.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECohen8_March_2012-79"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Condition_and_deterioration_of_the_wreck">Condition and deterioration of the wreck</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Wreck_of_the_RMS_Titanic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Condition and deterioration of the wreck">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rattail.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="245" data-file-width="600" height="90" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Rattail.jpg/220px-Rattail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Rattail.jpg/330px-Rattail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Rattail.jpg/440px-Rattail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rattail.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rattail or grenadier fish, typical of the deep-sea fauna around <i>Titanic</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Prior to the discovery of <i>Titanic</i> '​s wreck, in addition to the common assumption that she had sunk in one piece, it had been widely believed that conditions at 12,000 feet down would preserve the ship virtually intact. The water is bitterly cold at only about 1–2 °C (34–36 °F), there is no light and the high pressure was thought to be likely to lower oxygen and salinity levels to the point that organisms would not be able to gain a foothold on the wreck. <i>Titanic</i> would effectively be in a deep freeze.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButler1998214_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1998214-84"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup> The reality has turned out to be very different, and the ship has increasingly deteriorated since she sank in April 1912. Her gradual decay is due to a number of different processes – physical, chemical and biological.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoneJuly_2004_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoneJuly_2004-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> She is situated on an undulating, gently sloping area of seabed in a small canyon swept by the Western <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Boundary_Current&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Boundary Current (page does not exist)">Boundary Current</a>. Eddies from the current flow constantly across the wreck, scouring the sea bed and keeping sediment from building up over the hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGannonFebruary_1995_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGannonFebruary_1995-73"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup> The current is strong and often changeable, gradually opening up holes in the ship's hull.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandwerk18_August_2010_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandwerk18_August_2010-86"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup> Salt corrosion eats away at the hull<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoneJuly_2004_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoneJuly_2004-85"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> and it is also affected by <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_corrosion" title="Galvanic corrosion">galvanic corrosion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHandwerk18_August_2010_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHandwerk18_August_2010-86"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The most dramatic deterioration has been caused by biological factors.
